Proofs for the Imamate of Uthman
Author presents rational and transmitted proofs establishing Uthman's legitimate caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple rational and transmitted proofs that establish the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate according to classical Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Uthman's imamate is proven by conditions, shura inclusion, and consensus.

This passage presents a comprehensive argument for the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate. The author builds on the established imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar, then enumerates the conditions of imamate and shows they were present in Uthman. He then provides several proofs: the contract by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, inclusion in the shura council, and the consensus of the companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of imamate
Read the passage carefully and list the conditions of imamate that the author says were present in Uthman: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability, etc.
These conditions are the foundation for the subsequent proofs.
2
Examine the proof from Abd al-Rahman's contract
The author argues that Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf was qualified to contract the caliphate, and he contracted it for Uthman. Understand why this is a proof.
The logic is: a qualified person would not contract for an unqualified person.
3
Understand the shura and companion silence proofs
Uthman's inclusion in the shura council by Umar implies his qualification. The companions' silence (not objecting) indicates their approval.
The companions are known for enjoining good and forbidding evil; their silence is significant.
4
Recognize the role of consensus
The author adds that the contract was accompanied by the consensus of the companions, which provides certain knowledge.
Consensus (ijma) is a strong proof in Sunni theology.
5
Analyze the reported interaction with Ali
The passage ends with a report where Abd al-Rahman offers the caliphate to Ali on condition of ruling by Quran, Sunnah, and the way of the two shaykhs. Consider its purpose in the argument.
This supports the legitimacy of the process and the condition of following the precedents of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة عثمان
وإذا ثبت إمامة الشيخين - رضي الله عنهما - بما ذكرنا من الدلائل ثبتت إمامة عثمان رضي الله عنه، لأن جميع شرائط الإمامة من النسب والعلم والزهد والقدرة على القيام بجميع ما يُحتاج إلى الإمام لأجله ثابتة في حقه، وقد عقد له عبد الرحمن بن عوف الخلافة وهو أهل للعقد بدليل اشتغال عبد الرحمن بن عوف له بالعقد له ولو لم يكن أهلاً لما فعل، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، ولو لم يكن أهلاً للخلافة لما أدخله عمر رضي الله عنه في أهل الشورى، ولو فعل ذلك لأنكره الصحابة إذ هم الموصوفون بتغيير المنكر، وحيث لم ينكروا، دل أنه كان أهلاً لذلك، ولأن وجد في غيره من شرائط الإمامة من النسب، والعلم بالحلال والحرام، وحفظ القرآن والعدالة والاستقلال بكفاية ما يناط بالإمام، وشرعة الخلافة له كانت ثابتة، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثم عقد من هو أهل للعقد ينعقد ضرورة، كيف وقد انضم إلى عقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف إجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم - وهو دليل موجب للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وما روى أن عبد الرحمن قال لعلي - رضي الله عنهما – أوليك على أن تحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وسيرة الشيخين فقال علي: أحكم بكتاب الله جميع ما
Discussion on the Imamate of Uthman. If the imamate of the two shaykhs (may Allah be pleased with them) is established by the proofs we have mentioned, then the imamate of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is established, because all the conditions of imamate: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, and ability to carry out all that is needed for the imam, are established in his case. And Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf contracted the caliphate for him, and he was qualified for the contract, as evidenced by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's engaging in contracting it for him. If he had not been qualified, he would not have done so. And because he was one of the people of the shura (consultative council). If he had not been qualified for the caliphate, Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) would not have included him among the people of the shura. And if he had done that, the companions would have objected, for they are described as changing the wrong. Since they did not object, it indicates that he was qualified for that. And because other conditions of imamate were found in him: lineage, knowledge of the lawful and unlawful, memorization of the Quran, justice, and independence in handling what is entrusted to the imam. And the legality of the caliphate for him was established. If that is established, then when one who is qualified for the contract contracts it, it is necessarily concluded. Moreover, the consensus of the companions (may Allah be pleased with them) was added to Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's contract, and it is a proof that necessitates certain and definitive knowledge. And it is reported that Abd al-Rahman said to Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both): 'I give you the pledge on condition that you rule by the Book of Allah, the Sunnah of His Messenger, and the way of the two shaykhs.' Ali said: 'I rule by the Book of Allah in all that...'
Source pages p. 1158

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument establishing the legitimacy of Uthman's imamate. The author begins by referencing the already-proven imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar. Then he enumerates the conditions of imamate (lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability) and asserts they were present in Uthman. He provides multiple proofs: (1) Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, who was qualified, contracted the caliphate for Uthman. (2) Uthman was included in the shura council by Umar, implying qualification. (3) The companions did not object to his inclusion, indicating approval. (4) Uthman possessed all required attributes. (5) The contract by a qualified person necessarily becomes valid, especially when accompanied by the consensus of the companions. Finally, the author cites a report where Abd al-Rahman offered the caliphate to Ali on condition of ruling by Quran, Sunnah, and the way of the two shaykhs, and Ali's response is cut off but implies acceptance of the condition.

الشورى
العقد
إجماع الصحابة

Key Takeaway
Uthman's imamate is firmly established through conditions, shura, contract, and consensus, leaving no room for doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The companions of the Prophet (peace be upon him) did not object to Uthman's inclusion in the shura. Do you have the same level of trust in your community's leaders, or do you find yourself quick to doubt their qualifications? What would it take for you to submit to a decision you initially disagree with?
?
Uthman's caliphate is proven by multiple clear evidences. Is your faith in the legitimacy of the early caliphate built on such rigorous proofs, or have you accepted it without examination? Could you defend it if someone challenged you?
?
Consider the condition of 'ability to carry out all that is needed for the imam.' In your own life, do you honestly assess whether you are capable of fulfilling the responsibilities you take on, or do you assume good intentions are enough? How might this lesson change your approach to leadership?

Refuting the Rafidah's View of the Companions
Imam Nasafi condemns those who adopt the Rafidah's negative view of the Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Nasafi condemns the Rafidah's interpretation of the Companions and the theological basis for their criticism.

Core Idea
To slander the Companions is to slander Allah's choice and invite His curse.

This brief but forceful passage from Imam Nasafi tackles a sensitive sectarian issue: the negative view some hold of the Companions. He anchors his refutation in a Quranic promise and makes clear that such views stem from corrupt belief. Let's see how he uses divine testimony to defend the honor of the Prophet's companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Note the Quranic quotation at the beginning and the strong language that follows.
The passage is short but dense with theological implications.
2
Identify the Quranic reference
Imam Nasafi quotes part of Quran 24:55. Understand its context: Allah promises to grant succession to believers who do righteous deeds. The author uses this to establish the high status of the Companions.
3
Analyze the condemnation
Notice how the author characterizes those who adopt the Rafidah view: 'bad opinion and corrupt belief.' He then lists the qualities of the Companions that they are denying: chosen by Allah, supporters of His religion, companions of the Prophet, and recipients of divine praise.
4
Reflect on the warning
The passage ends with a warning: Allah will repay such people for their bad opinion and corrupt belief, and invokes a curse upon them. Consider the severity of this language in the context of Islamic theology.
This reflects the importance of proper reverence for the Companions in Sunni doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امَنُوا مِنكُمْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَاتِ لَيَسْتَخْلِفَنَّهُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ فمن حمل أمرهم على ما حملته الروافض؛ فذلك لسوء رأيه وفساد اعتقاده فيمن اختاره الله عز وجل لنصرة دينه، وصحبة نبيه عليه السلام، وأثنى عليه بالخير، ووصفه بكل جميل، والله تعالى مجازيهم ومكافئهم على سوء رأيهم وفساد اعتقادهم فيهم، لعنهم الله.
Those who have believed among you and done righteous deeds - He will surely make them successors in the land. So whoever interprets their matter as the Rafidah interpret it, that is due to his bad opinion and corrupt belief regarding those whom Allah Almighty chose for supporting His religion, accompanying His Prophet (peace be upon him), and whom He praised with goodness and described with every beautiful quality. And Allah Almighty will repay them and requite them for their bad opinion and corrupt belief concerning them, may Allah curse them.
Source pages p. 1158

Commentary
This passage is a condemnation of those who hold the Rafidah's negative view of the Companions. It begins with a partial Quranic verse (Quran 24:55) about succession. The author argues that those who adopt such views have a bad opinion and corrupt belief regarding the Companions, whom Allah chose to support His religion and accompany Prophet Muhammad, and whom He praised. Therefore, such individuals deserve Allah's curse.

Key Terms
الروافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the Quranic verse (24:55) supports the Rafidah's criticism of the Companions because it speaks of succession after believers.
Correction: Imam Nasafi uses the same verse to affirm the Companions' high status, arguing that the Rafidah's interpretation stems from corrupt belief, not from the verse itself.

Key Takeaway
Criticizing the Companions is not merely a difference of opinion but a sign of corrupt belief that incurs divine displeasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear or read criticism of any Companion, even quietly, what is the state of your heart? Does it recoil in defense of Allah's chosen ones, or does it entertain doubts? This text warns that such doubts are the root of corrupt belief. How will you guard your heart against them starting today?
?
The Rafidah claim to love Ahl al-Bayt, yet they slander many Companions. Has your own love for any group ever led you to unjustly criticize others? This lesson calls you to examine if your loyalties align with Allah's praise. Will you repent from any such tendencies and adopt the full love for all whom Allah has praised?
?
Allah says He will repay those with bad opinions about the Companions. Do you truly believe that slander against the Companions is not a minor issue but incurs divine curse? How does this awareness change the way you speak about the Companions today—will you commit to defending them whenever their honor is attacked?

Proof of the Imamate of Uthman ibn Affan
Imam Nasafi argues for the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate using multiple proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple proofs Imam Nasafi uses to establish the legitimacy of Uthman's caliphate, including the conditions of imamate, the role of shura, and the consensus of the Companions.

Core Idea
Uthman's imamate is proven by the same conditions as Abu Bakr and Umar, plus valid contract, shura, and consensus.

Imam Nasafi continues his defense of the rightly-guided caliphs. After establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr and Umar, he now turns to Uthman. This passage presents a cumulative argument: Uthman possessed all the necessary qualities, was chosen by qualified electors, and his caliphate was confirmed by the silent consensus of the Companions. The author also includes a narrated exchange that underscores the Companions' reverence for the first two caliphs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of imamate
Read the passage and list the conditions Nasafi mentions: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability. Notice that he claims all are present in Uthman.
These conditions are the standard requirements in Sunni political thought.
2
Examine the first proof: Abd al-Rahman's contract
The author argues that Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf was qualified to contract the caliphate, and his doing so for Uthman proves Uthman's worthiness.
The text says: 'if he were not qualified, he would not have done it.'
3
Consider the shura argument
Umar included Uthman in the consultative council. If Uthman were unqualified, Umar would not have done so, and the Companions would have objected. Their silence indicates approval.
The Companions are described as 'changing the wrong' – they would have spoken up if Uthman were unfit.
4
Understand the consensus (ijma')
The consensus of the Companions added to Abd al-Rahman's contract yields certain knowledge. This is a strong proof in Sunni kalam.
Consensus (ijma') is considered a source of definitive knowledge.
5
Analyze the narrated story
The story of the pledge shows that Abu Bakr and Umar's caliphate was highly regarded. Ali's conditional acceptance is used to affirm their legitimacy, not oppose Uthman.
This story also reinforces the authority of the first two caliphs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام في إمامة عثمان وإذا ثبت إمامة الشيخين - رضي الله عنهما - بما ذكرنا من الدلائل ثبتت إمامة عثمان الله، لأن جميع شرائط الإمامة من النسب والعلم والزهد والقدرة على القيام بجميع ما يُحتاج إلى الإمام لأجله ثابتة في حقه، وقد عقد له عبد الرحمن بن عوف الخلافة وهو أهل للعقد بدليل اشتغال عبد الرحمن بن عوف له بالعقد له ولو لم يكن أهلاً لما فعل، ولأنه أحد أهل الشورى، ولو لم يكن أهلاً للخلافة لما أدخله عمر لاله لاله في أهل الشورى، ولو فعل ذلك لأنكره الصحابة إذ هم الموصوفون بتغيير المنكر، وحيث لم ينكروا، دل أنه كان أهلاً لذلك، ولأن وجد في غيره من شرائط الإمامة من النسب، والعلم بالحلال والحرام، وحفظ القرآن والعدالة والاستقلال بكفاية ما يناط بالإمام، وشرعة الخلافة له كانت ثابتة، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثم عقد من هو أهل للعقد ينعقد ضرورة، كيف وقد انضم إلى عقد عبد الرحمن بن عوف إجماع الصحابة - رضي الله عنهم الله عنهم - وهو دليل موجب للعلم قطعا ويقينا، وما روى أن عبد الرحمن قال لعلي - رضي الله عنهما – أوليك على أن تحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وسيرة الشيخين فقال علي: أحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله وأجتهد رأيي، فقال ذلك لعثمان، فقال: نعم. دليل على صحة خلافة الشيخين واعتقاد الصحابة إمامتهما، وأنهم كانوا يحمدون طريقهما، ويقتفون آثارهما، ويسلكون سبيلهما، ويرضون بسيرتهما.
Discussion on the Imamate of Uthman. And if the imamate of the two shaykhs (may Allah be pleased with them) is established by the proofs we have mentioned, then the imamate of Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) is established, because all the conditions of imamate – lineage, knowledge, asceticism, and ability to carry out all that is needed for the imam – are established in his right. And Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf contracted the caliphate for him, and he was qualified for the contract, as evidenced by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's undertaking the contract for him; and if he were not qualified, he would not have done it. And because he was one of the people of the shura (consultative council), and if he were not qualified for the caliphate, Umar would not have included him among the people of the shura; and if he had done that, the Companions would have objected, for they are described as changing the wrong, and since they did not object, that indicates that he was qualified for it. And because in him were found other conditions of imamate: lineage, knowledge of the lawful and unlawful, memorization of the Quran, justice, and independence in sufficient management of what is entrusted to the imam, and the legality of the caliphate for him was established. And if that is established, then a contract by one who is qualified for the contract necessarily becomes binding. How [can it be otherwise] when the consensus of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) was added to the contract of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf, which is a proof that yields certain and definite knowledge. And what is narrated that Abd al-Rahman said to Ali (may Allah be pleased with them both): 'I pledge to you to govern by the Book of Allah, the Sunna of His Messenger, and the sira of the two shaykhs (Abu Bakr and Umar).' So Ali said: 'I will govern by the Book of Allah, the Sunna of His Messenger, and I will exercise my own ijtihad.' Then he said that to Uthman, and he said: 'Yes.' This is evidence of the validity of the caliphate of the two shaykhs and the Companions' belief in their imamate, and that they used to praise their way, follow their traces, walk in their path, and be pleased with their sira.
Source pages p. 1158

Commentary
This passage is a proof for Uthman's imamate. The author lists the conditions of imamate (lineage, knowledge, asceticism, ability) and argues they are present in Uthman. He then presents multiple proofs: 1) Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf's contract, which is valid because he was qualified; 2) Umar's inclusion of Uthman in the shura council, which shows his qualification, and the Companions' silence confirms it; 3) the presence of other conditions; 4) the consensus (ijma') of the Companions, which provides certainty; 5) the narrated story of the pledge, which demonstrates the Companions' high regard for the caliphate of Abu Bakr and Umar.

Key Terms
إمامة
الشورى
إجماع
الخلفاء الراشدين
شرائط الإمامة
عبد الرحمن بن عوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's conditional response shows he did not fully accept Uthman's caliphate.
Correction: The author uses the story to show that the Companions admired Abu Bakr and Umar's sira, and Ali's response does not indicate opposition to Uthman but rather his independence in ijtihad.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the inclusion of Uthman by Umar in the shura council indicate?
A) That Uthman was not qualified.
B) That Uthman was qualified for the caliphate.
C) That the Companions objected.
D) That the caliphate is hereditary.

Key Takeaway
Through conditions, contract, shura, consensus, and a historical example, Nasafi demonstrates that Uthman's caliphate is as legitimate as those of his predecessors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi lists four conditions for imamate: lineage, knowledge, asceticism, and ability. Which of these four do you lack most in your own role of leadership—whether in your family, community, or workplace? What specific step can you take this week to strengthen that quality?
?
The Companions remained silent when Uthman was included in the shura, trusting Umar's judgment. Think of a time when you questioned a leadership decision in your community. Did your reaction reflect trust in the collective wisdom of those in authority, or did it come from personal bias? What would it look like to trust the process of shura today?
?
When Ali was asked to govern by the sira of Abu Bakr and Umar, he added 'I will exercise my own ijtihad.' Uthman accepted unconditionally. In your daily life, do you tend to follow the established path of the righteous predecessors unconditionally, or do you feel the need to adapt it? What is the real motivation behind your choice—convenience, conviction, or pride?

Ali's Statement: Opposition or Ijtihad Methodology?
Imam Nasafi explains that Ali's conditional acceptance does not indicate opposition but a difference in ijtihad methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ali's conditional acceptance of the pledge reflects a legitimate difference in ijtihad methodology, not opposition to the earlier caliphs.

Core Idea
Ali's ijtihad condition proves unity, not division.

Imam Nasafi addresses an objection: did Ali's statement 'I will exercise my own ijtihad' show his opposition to Abu Bakr and Umar? The author clarifies that this was simply a methodological difference among mujtahids. Let's examine the reasoning and the two schools of thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the counter-argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first states the objection (Ali's statement) and then immediately refutes it with prior proofs of Ali's allegiance.
The objection is based on a surface reading of Ali's words.
2
Understand why the objection is invalid
The author argues that the objection fails because there are many proofs that Ali followed and was pleased with the previous caliphs. Ali's condition is not evidence of opposition.
3
Examine the two schools of ijtihad methodology
The passage explains that Ali held the view that a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad and cannot imitate others. The other view (of Abd al-Rahman and Uthman) allows imitation of a more knowledgeable person. This difference does not indicate enmity.
Both views are considered valid within Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider how this explanation defends the unity of the Companions and shows that differences in legal methodology are natural and not signs of division.
This is a key point in Sunni polemics against those who claim early divisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقول علي الله الله : أحكم بكتاب الله وسنة رسوله عليه السلام وأجتهد رأيي، ليس بدليل على مخالفته لهما ومجانبته إياهما لما مر من الدلائل الدالة على متابعته إياهما ورضاه بإمامتهما، بل ذلك لأن مذهبه كان أن المجتهد يجب عليه اتباع اجتهاده، ولا يجوز له تقليد غير من المجتهدين، وكان مذهب عبد الرحمن بن عوف وعثمان أن المجتهد يجوز أن يقلد غيره إذا كان أفقه منه، وأعلم بطرق الدين وأبصر بوجوه المقاييس، وأن يترك اجتهاد نفسه ورأيه ويتبع رأي ذلك، وبقى هذا الخلاف في أئمة الدين وفقهاء الأمة.
And the statement of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him): 'I will govern by the Book of Allah, the Sunna of His Messenger, and I will exercise my own ijtihad' is not evidence of his opposing them and avoiding them, due to the proofs that have passed indicating his following them and his pleasure with their imamate. Rather, that is because his school was that the mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, and it is not permissible for him to imitate another of the mujtahids. And the school of Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf and Uthman was that the mujtahid may imitate another if that other is more jurisprudentially skilled than him, more knowledgeable in the ways of religion, and more insightful in the aspects of analogical reasoning, and that he should leave his own ijtihad and opinion and follow the opinion of that person. And this difference remained among the imams of the religion and the jurists of the community.
Source pages p. 1159

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection is that Ali's condition to use his own ijtihad shows he opposed the two shaykhs. The answer is that it is not opposition; rather, it reflects a legitimate difference in the methodology of ijtihad. Ali held that a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad and cannot imitate others. The opposing view (of Abd al-Rahman and Uthman) allows imitation of a more knowledgeable person. This difference is well-known among scholars and does not indicate any ill will.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد
التقليد
المجتهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Ali's insistence on his own ijtihad indicates he rejected the authority of the previous caliphs.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is a methodological difference: Ali believed a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, which does not imply opposition.

Checkpoint
Why did Ali condition his pledge with 'I will exercise my own ijtihad' according to the passage?
A) Because he opposed the two shaykhs.
B) Because he believed a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad.
C) Because he wanted to delay the pledge.
D) Because he was not qualified.

Key Takeaway
Ali's conditional acceptance was based on his principle that a mujtahid must follow his own ijtihad, a legitimate methodological difference, not a sign of opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious question, do you genuinely strive to understand the evidence yourself, or do you simply follow what others say out of convenience? This lesson shows that even great Companions differed on whether a scholar must personally reason or may rely on a more expert one. Which approach reflects your current habit, and what would it take for you to take greater responsibility for your own understanding?
?
Ali (r) insisted on following his own ijtihad even when it meant differing from the majority view. Can you think of a time in your life when you stood firm on a conviction after sincere research, despite pressure to conform? If you haven't, what fears prevent you from doing so? Meanwhile, what about humility? How do you balance conviction with openness to correction?

Warning Against Rejecting Any Rightly Guided Caliph
Nasafi defends the status of all Rashidun caliphs and criticizes relying on weak narrations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rejecting any of the four Rightly Guided Caliphs contradicts the consensus of the Companions and why weak solitary narrations are not acceptable against that consensus.

Core Idea
To reject a Rightly Guided Caliph is to reject the consensus of the Companions.

Imam Nasafi responds to those who would diminish any of the Rashidun caliphs. He first clarifies Ali's own stance toward Uthman, then broadens the argument. He explains that rejecting any caliph implies that the Companions collectively agreed on something evil, which is impossible. He also warns against relying on weak solitary reports (ahad) that contradict the established consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the evidence from Ali
Notice that Nasafi uses Ali's allegiance to Uthman as evidence that Ali's earlier position was not opposition. This sets the stage for defending all caliphs.
3
Appreciate the argument about consensus
Nasafi argues that rejecting any caliph amounts to accusing the Companions of agreeing upon evil and not correcting it—thus removing the force of their consensus.
This is a powerful argument from the authority of ijma.
4
Consider the critique of ahad reports
Most narrations against a caliph are solitary (ahad) and rely on untrustworthy transmitters. They cannot overrule the consensus of the Companions.
5
Reflect on the priority of ijma
Nasafi concludes that adhering to the consensus of the Companions is more appropriate than following anomalous narrations. This establishes a hierarchy of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على أن ذلك كان لاختلاف فيما بينهما في هذه المسألة دون أن يرى على مخالفتهما : أنه بايع عثمان لله واعتقد إمامته، وكان يساعده في أموره. والحاصل أن كل من يأبى خلافة أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين أو ينسب واحدا منهم إلى ما لا يحل أو إلى ما يوجب قدحا في حاله، فهو ممن يزيل إجماع الصحابة عن كونه حجة عليه، ويصفهم بالإجماع على ما هو المنكر، والامتناع عن تغييره، وأكثر ما يروون ما يوجب قدحا في واحد منهم أخبار تثبت بطريق الآحاد، ومدار ذلك كله على من لا يوثق به فلا يكون مقبولاً بمقابلة إجماع الصحابة، وكثير مما يروون أشياء لا حجة لهم فيها، ويظنونها بجهالتهم بمخارجها ووجوهها، حجة لهم، والتمسك بإجماع الصحابة أولى من اتباع مثل تلك الروايات الشاذة الخارجة عن
And the evidence that that was due to a difference between them in this issue, not to his [i.e., Ali's] opposing them, is that he pledged allegiance to Uthman and believed in his imamate, and used to assist him in his affairs. The conclusion is that whoever rejects the caliphate of any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs, or attributes to any of them something that is not permissible, or something that necessitates a defect in his state, he is one who removes the consensus of the Companions from being a proof against him, and describes them as having agreed upon that which is objectionable and refrained from changing it. Most of what they narrate that entails a defect in any of them are reports established through solitary narrations (ahad), and the entire chain of such reports depends on those who are not trustworthy, so it is not acceptable against the consensus of the Companions. Much of what they narrate are things that have no evidence for them, and they think out of ignorance of their interpretations and aspects that they are evidence. And adherence to the consensus of the Companions is more appropriate than following such anomalous narrations that deviate from...
Source pages p. 1159

Commentary
This passage is a general refutation. It first provides evidence that Ali's stance was not opposition, because he actually pledged allegiance and helped Uthman. Then it broadens to condemn anyone who rejects any of the Rashidun caliphs. Such a person effectively claims that the Companions were unanimous in an evil and did not correct it. The reports they cite are weak solitary narrations (ahad) from unreliable transmitters. It is better to hold to the consensus (ijma') of the Companions than to follow these anomalous reports.

Key Terms
الخلفاء الراشدين
الآحاد
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any narration attacking a Rightly Guided Caliph is acceptable evidence against him.
Correction: Such narrations are typically weak (ahad) from unreliable transmitters and cannot overrule the strong consensus of the Companions.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of narrations that attack a Rightly Guided Caliph?
A) They are always accepted.
B) They are mostly weak (ahad) and based on unreliable transmitters.
C) They are stronger than consensus.
D) They are from the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions is a stronger proof than isolated narrations, and rejecting any Rightly Guided Caliph undermines that consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed the consensus of the learned community because of an isolated report that suited your opinion? What does that reveal about your trust in the collective wisdom of the Companions?
?
When you hear criticisms of a rightly guided caliph, do you instinctively assume there is some truth to it, or do you hold the consensus of the Sahaba as the default position? Will you examine the chain of narrations before accepting such claims from now on?
?
Consider your attitude towards the Sahaba: do you give them the benefit of the doubt, or do you allow weak narrations to sow seeds of doubt in your heart? What change will you make to guard your heart and tongue?

Rejecting the Caliphs Undermines Ijma'
Argues that rejecting any caliph invalidates Ijma' and relies on weak solitary reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rejecting any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs undermines the authority of the Companions' consensus and why weak solitary reports cannot outweigh it.

Core Idea
Denying a caliph's legitimacy strips the Companions' consensus of its authority.

This passage establishes a key principle: the consensus (ijma') of the Companions is a definitive proof in Islam. The author argues that anyone who rejects or criticizes a Rightly Guided Caliph is effectively dismissing that consensus and relying on unreliable solitary reports. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the argument.
Notice the strong language: 'removes the consensus from being a proof'.
2
Identify the central claim
Find the sentence that states what happens when someone rejects a caliph. The author says such a person 'removes the consensus (ijma') of the Companions from being a proof against him.'
3
Examine the evidence used by critics
The passage mentions that critics rely on 'reports established by solitary transmission (khabar al-ahad)' from untrustworthy narrators. Notice why these are considered weak.
Solitary reports do not provide certainty and are outweighed by consensus.
4
Understand the conclusion
The author concludes that clinging to the ijma' of the Companions is more deserving than following such anomalous reports, especially in matters requiring knowledge ('ilm). Ask yourself why ijma' takes precedence.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن كل من يأبى خلافة أحد من الخلفاء الراشدين أو ينسب واحدا منهم إلى ما لا يحل أو إلى ما يوجب قدحا في حاله، فهو ممن يزيل إجماع الصحابة عن كونه حجة عليه، ويصفهم بالإجماع على ما هو المنكر، والامتناع عن تغييره، وأكثر ما يروون ما يوجب قدحا في واحد منهم أخبار تثبت بطريق الآحاد، ومدار ذلك كله على من لا يوثق به فلا يكون مقبولاً بمقابلة إجماع الصحابة، وكثير مما يروون أشياء لا حجة لهم فيها، ويظنونها بجهالتهم بمخارجها ووجوهها، حجة لهم، والتمسك بإجماع الصحابة أولى من اتباع مثل تلك الروايات الشاذة الخارجة عن إجماع المسلمين خصوصا فيما الحاجة فيه إلى ثبوت العلم دون العمل، وهي موجبة للعلم وإن خلت عن معارضة الإجماع، فكيف وقد وردت بمخالفته.
And the conclusion is that whoever rejects the caliphate of any of the Rightly Guided Caliphs or attributes to any one of them that which is not permissible or that which necessitates criticism of his state, then he is among those who remove the consensus (ijma') of the Companions from being a proof against him, and he describes them as agreeing upon that which is objectionable and refraining from changing it. And most of what they relate that necessitates criticism of any one of them are reports established by solitary transmission (khabar al-ahad). And the entire matter rests on those who are not trustworthy, so it is not acceptable in the face of the ijma' of the Companions. And much of what they relate are things in which they have no proof, but they imagine, due to their ignorance of their sources and aspects, that they are proofs for them. And clinging to the ijma' of the Companions is more deserving than following such anomalous reports that depart from the consensus of the Muslims, especially in matters where there is need for establishing knowledge ('ilm) rather than action ('amal). For such reports would necessitate knowledge even if they were free from opposition to ijma', so how [can they be accepted] when they have come in opposition to it?
Source pages p. 1159

Commentary
This passage is a general principle and refutation of those who reject the legitimate caliphs or accuse them. It asserts that if anyone denies the caliphate of any Rightly Guided Caliph or makes accusations against them, that person effectively denies the binding authority of the Companions' consensus (ijma'). The author explains that such individuals rely on solitary reports (khabar al-ahad) transmitted by untrustworthy narrators, which cannot outweigh the definitive consensus of all Companions. Moreover, many of these reports are misunderstood by their proponents due to ignorance of their proper interpretation (makharij and wujuh). The author emphasizes that holding to the ijma' of the Companions is superior to following such anomalous reports, especially in doctrinal matters ('ilm) where certainty is required. Even if solitary reports could theoretically provide knowledge, they are nullified when they contradict the consensus—and here they do contradict it.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة (ijma' al-sahaba)
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid)
شاذ (shadhdh)
قدح (qadh - criticism/disparagement)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any historical report criticizing a Companion is acceptable evidence against them.
Correction: Such reports are usually solitary and weak, and they cannot outweigh the definitive consensus of the Companions, which affirms their integrity.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that reports criticizing a caliph are not acceptable?
A) They are all fabricated
B) They contradict the consensus of the Companions and rely on weak chains
C) The caliphs were infallible and above criticism
D) Such reports only pertain to actions, not beliefs

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the Companions is a definitive proof that cannot be overturned by weak solitary reports, especially in matters of belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a Companion, do you pause to consider whether your acceptance of that criticism effectively undermines the consensus of the entire Sahaba? How will you train yourself to prioritize Ijma' over appealing but weak reports?

Is there any area of your belief where you have allowed isolated reports to weaken your trust in the collective integrity of the Sahaba? What step will you take today to reaffirm your commitment to their consensus?

Imam Uthman's Authority in the Case of al-Hurmuzan
Refutes Rawafid accusation by citing Islamic legal principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Uthman's decision not to execute Abdullah ibn Umar was legally justified under Islamic law, and how the Rawafid accusation is based on ignorance of the principles of blood guardianship.

Core Idea
When a victim has no heir, the Imam represents the community and may choose execution or blood money.

The Rawafid accused Uthman of failing to execute Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, but this overlooks the Islamic legal principle that when a victim has no heir, the Imam represents the community and can choose between execution or blood money. This passage clarifies that legal authority, not favoritism, guided Uthman's decision.

Guided Path
1
Understand the accusation
Read the passage to identify what the Rawafid claimed against Uthman. Note that they criticized him for not executing Abdullah ibn Umar despite the suspicion of involvement in Umar's assassination.
The phrase 'لِاتِّهَامِهِ إِيَّاهُ فِي قَتْلِ عُمَرَ' indicates the suspicion.
2
Grasp the legal principle
The passage explains that the victim al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the 'guardian' (wali al-dam) becomes the entire Muslim community. Understand that the Imam acts on their behalf.
3
Recognize the Imam's discretion
The Imam has the authority to either carry out execution or accept blood money (diya). This discretion is legally valid, so criticism of Uthman's choice is due to ignorance of the law.
The text ends with 'فَمَنْ طَعَنَ بِذَلِكَ فَلِجَهْلِهِ طَعَنَ'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما طعنت الروافض على عثمان الله أنه ترك قتل عبد الله بن عمر حين قتل الهرمزان (۲) لاتهامه إياه في قتل عمر الله، فطعن في غير مطعن وذلك لأن ولي الهرمزان جماعة المسلمين إذ لم يكن له ،وارث، والإمام هو القائم بأمور المسلمين فيكون هو المتصرف في استيفاء القتل (۳) أو (٤) العفو بالدية فمن طعن بذلك فلجهله طعن.
As for what the Rawafid have criticized Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, concerning his not executing Abdullah ibn Umar when he killed al-Hurmuzan (2) due to suspicion that he was involved in the killing of Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, then it is criticism in an invalid place. That is because the guardian of al-Hurmuzan was the congregation of Muslims since he had no heir, and the Imam is the one who manages the affairs of the Muslims, so he is the one who decides whether to carry out execution (3) or to pardon for blood money (4). So whoever criticizes that is criticizing out of ignorance.
Source pages p. 1160

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific accusation made by the Rawafid (a term for extreme Shi'a) against Caliph Uthman. The accusation is that Uthman failed to execute Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan, a Persian leader, on suspicion that he had participated in assassinating Umar. The author responds that this criticism is unfounded because the right to demand retaliation (qisas) belonged to the entire Muslim community, as al-Hurmuzan had no specific heir. The Imam (Uthman) was the representative of the community, so he had the authority to either execute the killer or accept blood money. Therefore, any objection to Uthman's decision stems from ignorance of Islamic legal principles regarding blood vengeance when the victim has no heir.

Key Terms
روافض
ولي الدم
دية
قتل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman was lenient on Abdullah ibn Umar out of favoritism or bias.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the decision was based on legal authority: al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the Imam (Uthman) could choose between execution or blood money as he saw fit.

Checkpoint
Why was Uthman not required to execute Abdullah ibn Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan?
A) Because Abdullah ibn Umar was a Companion
B) Because al-Hurmuzan was not a Muslim
C) Because al-Hurmuzan had no heir, and the Imam could decide between execution or blood money
D) Because Uthman was afraid of Abdullah's father

Key Takeaway
Uthman's decision was legally sound because the Imam represents the entire community as the wali al-dam when the victim has no heir.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear accusations against a Muslim leader or a Companion of the Prophet, do you investigate the full Islamic context before forming an opinion? Think of a time you judged someone without understanding their legal or ethical reasoning. What step will you take tomorrow to avoid that ignorance?
?
This lesson shows that the Imam's authority can override personal desires for revenge. Is there a situation in your life where you struggle to submit to legitimate Islamic authority or a ruling you find difficult? Write down one concrete action you will take to align your heart with the Shari'a even when it differs from your emotions.

Defending Uthman's Return of al-Hakam
Defends Uthman's decision to return al-Hakam ibn al-As to Medina

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind Uthman's decision to return al-Hakam ibn al-As to Medina and how it was justified according to Islamic teachings.

Core Idea
Uthman's return of al-Hakam was based on prophetic permission and the removal of the reason for exile.

This passage addresses a historical accusation against Caliph Uthman. It explains the reasoning behind his decision to allow al-Hakam ibn al-As to return to Medina after having been exiled by the Prophet. The author presents Uthman's defense, showing that the action was not a violation but justified by both prophetic permission and the fulfillment of kinship obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument.
Pay attention to the sequence: accusation, response, evidence.
2
Identify the accusation and the response
The accusation is that Uthman returned the Prophet's exile. Uthman's defense includes that he had permission, he informed Abu Bakr and Umar, and when he became caliph he acted on his own knowledge.
Note the role of witnesses: Abu Bakr and Umar required another witness.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
Uthman judged that the reason for exile had ceased and that maintaining kinship with his maternal uncle was obligatory. These two grounds justified the return.
The principle of 'ilm al-imam (knowledge of the imam) allowed him to rule based on his own knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this defense upholds Uthman's justice and the flexibility of Islamic law to adapt when causes change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: أنه رد الحكم بن العاص - طريد رسول الله عليه السلام - إلى المدينة، فهو أيضا ،فاسد، فإن عثمان الله أخبره أنه كان استأذن النبي عليه السلام في رده فأذن له، وقد كان أخبر بذلك أبا بكر الله فطالبه بشاهد آخر معه ليرده، وكذا فعل عمر الله، فلما وُلِّيَ هو بنفسه حكم بعلمه، على أن المعنى الذي كان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أخرجه له قد كان ارتفع وهو كان عما له، وكانت صلة قرابته واجبة عليه فرده صلة للقرابة مع علمه بزوال المعنى الموجب لطرده وإخراجه.
And what they said: that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-As, the exile of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), to Medina is also invalid. For Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him, informed [them] that he had sought permission from the Prophet (peace be upon him) to return him, and he (the Prophet) had permitted him. And he had informed Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him, of that, and Abu Bakr demanded another witness with him in order to return him. And similarly Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, did [the same]. But when he (Uthman) himself was in charge, he ruled by his own knowledge, based on the fact that the reason for which the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) had exiled him had been removed, and that he was his maternal uncle, and maintaining family ties was obligatory upon him. So he returned him as an act of maintaining kinship ('sila lil-qaraba') while knowing that the reason that necessitated his exile and expulsion had ceased.
Source pages p. 1160

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that Uthman wrongly brought back al-Hakam ibn al-As, who had been exiled by the Prophet Muhammad. The author presents Uthman's defense: Uthman claimed that the Prophet had permitted his return, though Abu Bakr and Umar had required a second witness to confirm this. When Uthman became caliph, he acted on his own knowledge (since he was the witness himself) and judged that the original reason for exile—likely al-Hakam's harmful behavior—had ended. Additionally, al-Hakam was Uthman's maternal uncle, so Uthman considered it an obligation to maintain family ties. Thus, his return was legally justified and not a violation of the Prophet's decree.

Key Terms

صلة القرابة (sila al-qaraba / maintaining kinship)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The passage shows the reason for exile had ended and the Prophet had given permission, so Uthman acted justly and not in defiance of the Prophet.

B) Because the reason for his exile had ended and Uthman knew the Prophet had permitted it

Uthman's return of al-Hakam was justified by prophetic permission, the cessation of the cause for exile, and the duty of maintaining kinship.

Refutation of the Accusation That Uthman's Governors Misbehaved
Refutes the claim that Uthman is blamed for the misconduct of some governors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the misconduct of some of Uthman's governors does not tarnish his leadership, based on his prompt dismissal of them.

Core Idea
A leader's character is judged by his actions, not by the crimes of those he removes.

This passage addresses a common historical criticism of Caliph Uthman: that some of his appointed governors committed misconduct. The author refutes this by showing that Uthman took corrective action, dismissing those governors once their betrayal was known. This lesson examines the logic of this defense and the example of al-Qa'qa' ibn Shur.

The example shows that individual betrayal occurred, but Uthman's response was corrective.

Refuting the Rafidi Objection on 'Uthman's Pardoning of Ibn 'Umar
Refuting the Rafidi objection using wali al-dam principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam 'Uthman's decision not to execute 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar was legally sound based on the principle that the Muslim community is the guardian of blood (wali al-dam) for a victim with no heir, and the Imam acts as their representative.

Core Idea
The Imam represents the community and may pardon or execute on behalf of a heirless victim.

The Rafidah criticized 'Uthman for not executing 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar after he killed al-Hurmuzan. This passage explains the Islamic legal reasoning: the victim had no heir, so the entire Muslim community becomes the guardian of his blood, and the Imam, as their representative, has the authority to choose punishment or pardon. Therefore, the criticism stemmed from ignorance of this fiqh principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the refutation with legal reasoning.
The passage is concise but packed with legal reasoning.
2
Identify the objection
Find what the Rafidah claimed against 'Uthman. They said he should have executed 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan due to suspicion about involvement in 'Umar's murder.
The objection is based on an incorrect premise according to the author.
3
Understand the legal basis of refutation
The author explains that al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the guardian of his blood (wali al-dam) is the Muslim community as a whole. The Imam acts on behalf of the community and has discretion to choose execution or accept blood-money (diyah).
This is a key Islamic legal principle regarding victims without heirs.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since 'Uthman's decision was within his lawful authority, the criticism is invalid and attributed to ignorance. Reflect on how understanding fiqh prevents unfair judgments against historical figures.
The lesson emphasizes evaluating actions based on Islamic law, not personal opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما ما طعنت الروافض على عثمان أنه ترك قتل عبد الله بن عمر حين قتل الهرمزان لاتهامه إياه في قتل عمر، فطعن في غير مطعن وذلك لأن ولي الهرمزان جماعة المسلمين إذ لم يكن له وارث، والإمام هو القائم بأمور المسلمين فيكون هو المتصرف في استيفاء القتل أو العفو بالدية فمن طعن بذلك فلجهله طعن.
As for what the Rafidah criticized 'Uthman for, that he refrained from killing 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar when he killed al-Hurmuzan due to his suspicion that he was involved in the killing of 'Umar, it is criticism on an incorrect basis. That is because the guardian of al-Hurmuzan was the community of Muslims since he had no heir, and the Imam is the one standing in charge of the affairs of the Muslims, so he is the one who has authority to execute the death penalty or to pardon with blood-money. So whoever criticizes on that basis does so out of ignorance.
Source pages p. 1160

Commentary
This passage refutes a Rafidi objection against Caliph 'Uthman. The objection claims that 'Uthman failed to execute 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar for killing al-Hurmuzan. The refutation first states that the objection is based on an incorrect premise. It then explains the legal reasoning: al-Hurmuzan had no heir, so the guardian of his blood (wali al-dam) is the entire Muslim community. The Imam acts as the representative of the community, thus he has the authority either to demand execution or to accept blood-money (diyah). Therefore, 'Uthman's decision was legally valid, and the criticism stems from ignorance of this Islamic legal principle.

Key Terms
الروافض
الهرمزان
ولي الدم
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Imam must execute the killer in all cases of murder, and 'Uthman's failure to do so was a mistake.
Correction: The Imam is not obligated to execute when the victim has no heir; the community (represented by the Imam) has the option of retribution or pardon with blood-money.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that 'Uthman was not at fault for not executing 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar?
A) Because 'Uthman was not the rightful Imam.
B) Because the victim had no heir, so the community, represented by the Imam, had the choice of execution or pardon.
C) Because 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar was a companion and immune from punishment.
D) Because 'Uthman did not have the authority to execute anyone.

Key Takeaway
Imam 'Uthman's decision was legally correct because the victim's guardian was the Muslim community, and the Imam has authority to pardon or execute on their behalf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a criticism of a historical figure like 'Uthman, do you immediately accept it or do you seek to understand the legal and contextual factors? This lesson shows that a seemingly damning accusation collapses once the full legal framework is known. Where in your own life have you judged someone without understanding their circumstances? Will you take the time to learn before forming opinions?
?
The Imam was entrusted with the authority to pardon on behalf of the community. Do you trust the legitimate authorities in your life to make right decisions, or do you assume the worst? This lesson reminds us that Islamic leadership is bound by law, not whim. What leader have you been too hasty to criticize? Commit to learning their side before passing judgment.
?

This lesson challenges you to study the foundations of Islamic jurisprudence so that your judgments are rooted in knowledge, not emotion. What specific area of fiqh will you learn next?

Refutation of the Objection on Uthman's Return of al-Hakam
Refuting the objection that Uthman wrongfully returned al-Hakam ibn al-As

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold justification for Uthman's return of al-Hakam ibn al-As and how it refutes the objection that he violated the Prophet's decree.

Core Idea
Uthman's return of al-Hakam was justified by prophetic permission, change of circumstances, and kinship duty.

This passage addresses a common objection against Caliph Uthman. Critics claim he wrongfully returned a man the Prophet had exiled. The author presents three arguments to show the return was lawful, based on prophetic permission, the cessation of the reason for exile, and the obligation of maintaining kinship ties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow of the refutation.
The author argues from multiple angles: permission, precedent, and changed circumstances.
2
Identify the three justifications
List the three distinct arguments Uthman used to justify his action. Understand how each one addresses the objection.
These are: (1) Prophet's permission, (2) informing Abu Bakr and Umar, (3) cessation of reason and kinship duty.
3
Reflect on the principle of changed rulings
Consider that in Islamic law, rulings based on a specific cause may change when the cause ceases. This passage exemplifies that principle.
The Prophet's exile was for a reason; when that reason ended, the ruling could be revisited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قالوا: أنه رد الحكم بن العاص طريد رسول الله إلى المدينة، فهو أيضا فاسد، فإن عثمان أخبره أنه كان استأذن النبي في رده فأذن له، وقد كان أخبر بذلك أبا بكر فطالبه بشاهد آخر معه ليرده، وكذا فعل عمر، فلما وُلِّيَ هو بنفسه حكم بعلمه، على أن المعنى الذي كان النبي أخرجه له قد كان ارتفع وهو كان عما له، وكانت صلة قرابته واجبة عليه فرده صلة للقرابة مع علمه بزوال المعنى الموجب لطرده وإخراجه.
And what they said: that he returned al-Hakam ibn al-'As, the exile of the Messenger of Allah, to Medina, is also invalid. For 'Uthman informed that he had sought permission from the Prophet to return him and he permitted him. He had informed Abu Bakr of that, but Abu Bakr demanded another witness with him to return him, and similarly 'Umar did. When 'Uthman himself became caliph, he ruled by his own knowledge. Moreover, the reason for which the Prophet had exiled him had ceased, and he had an obligation to maintain kinship ties, so he returned him as an act of maintaining kinship, while knowing that the reason necessitating his expulsion had ceased.
Source pages p. 1160

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that 'Uthman wrongfully returned al-Hakam ibn al-'As, whom the Prophet had exiled. The refutation proceeds in steps: First, 'Uthman states that he had asked the Prophet for permission to return him, and the Prophet granted it. Second, 'Uthman had informed Abu Bakr and 'Umar of this, but they required a second witness; 'Uthman, as caliph, acted on his own knowledge. Third, the underlying reason for the exile had ceased, and 'Uthman's act was one of maintaining kinship ties, which is obligatory. Thus the return was justified.

Key Terms

طريد
الصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the Prophet's decree to exile al-Hakam was permanent and irrevocable, so any return is a violation.
Correction: The passage shows that the decree was based on a reason that ceased to exist, and the Prophet himself had permitted the return. Thus, Uthman's action was lawful.

Checkpoint

C) Because al-Hakam was a close relative and the Prophet had revoked the exile.
D) Because the community demanded his return.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's return of al-Hakam was justified by prophetic permission, the lifting of the cause of exile, and the Islamic duty of maintaining kinship ties.

Uthman's Dismissal of Corrupt Governors Defends Him
Refutation of the claim that Uthman's governors' misdeeds tarnish his reputation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the misconduct of some of Uthman's governors does not reflect negatively on him, because he dismissed them upon learning of it.

Core Idea
A caliph is not blamed for a governor's betrayal if he dismisses him.

This lesson addresses a common objection against Caliph Uthman: that some of his appointed governors were corrupt or treacherous. The author argues that Uthman's immediate dismissal of those governors upon discovery of their misconduct absolves him of any blame. Thus, the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: claim, refutation, examples.
The Arabic uses a strong refutation tone with 'fasid aydan'.
2
Identify the two examples
The author mentions al-Qa'qa' bin Shur and al-Ash'ath bin Qays. They were appointed over Maysan and Adharbayjan and betrayed, but Uthman dismissed them.
These historical examples show that betrayal happened but did not tarnish Uthman.
3
Understand the principle
The key principle is that a leader is not responsible for a subordinate's hidden betrayal if he acts justly by removing them. This refutes the objection.
The author emphasizes that dismissal prevents criticism ('la yujibu qadhan').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أن بعض عماله خانوا وظهرت منهم أمور مستكرهة فاسد أيضا، لأنه لما ظهر ذلك من ولاته عزلهم، وذلك لا يوجب قدحا فيه ولا وهنا في حاله، فإن القعقاع بن شور ولاه على ميسان فأخذ أموالها ولحق بمعاوية، وكذا حال أشعث بن قيس حين ولاه أذربيجان فاختان فيها، ولم يوجب طعنا.
And what they claim that some of his governors betrayed and reprehensible matters appeared from them is also invalid, because when that appeared from his governors, he dismissed them. And that does not necessitate criticism against him nor weakness in his situation. For al-Qa'qa' bin Shur, he appointed him over Maysan, so he took its wealth and joined Mu'awiyah. And similarly the case of al-Ash'ath bin Qays when he appointed him over Adharbayjan, and he embezzled there, and it did not necessitate any criticism.
Source pages p. 1160

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that some of 'Uthman's governors were corrupt. The refutation argues that 'Uthman acted appropriately by dismissing them as soon as their misconduct became known. The fact that some governors betrayed does not reflect on 'Uthman's character or leadership. Two examples are given: al-Qa'qa' bin Shur in Maysan and al-Ash'ath bin Qays in Adharbayjan. Both were removed, and this does not constitute a valid criticism against 'Uthman.

أشعث بن قيس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A caliph is responsible for every action of his appointees, including hidden betrayal.
Correction: In Islamic political thought, a leader is only blameworthy if he knowingly keeps corrupt governors. Uthman dismissed them, so he is not to blame.

Checkpoint

B) By saying that 'Uthman was unaware of their actions.
C) By stating that 'Uthman dismissed them once their misconduct was known, so it does not reflect on him.
D) By arguing that the governors were appointed by previous caliphs.

Key Takeaway
Uthman is not to blame for the misconduct of his governors because he dismissed them as soon as their betrayal was known.

Why Uthman Burned the Quranic Codices
Refuting the claim that Uthman wrongfully burned the Quranic texts by reason of benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational justification for Uthman's burning of the Quranic codices as an act of preservation based on communal benefit and consensus.

Core Idea
What seems drastic can be necessary for preservation.

This passage tackles a historical objection against Caliph Uthman. Critics claim his burning of Quranic codices was a wrongful destruction of divine revelation. The author responds by framing the act as a precautionary measure to ensure uniformity and prevent confusion. Through a simple analogy, he shows that such actions are not only permissible but praiseworthy when they serve the greater good.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Understand the claim that Uthman burned codices and the author's defense: it was to prevent readings outside the Companions' consensus.
2
Consider the analogy
Why does the author compare it to washing slates in a school? What does that teach about the principle of maslahah (public benefit)?
3
Reflect on broader significance
How does this defense rely on the authority of ijmāʿ (consensus) and the priority of communal interest over individual copies?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه أحرق المصاحف فذلك لئلا يقرأ إنسان بغير ما أجمعت الصحابة على ثبوته، ومثل ذلك لمصلحة ما لا يكون منكرا كغسل الألواح في المكاتب.
And what they claim that he burned the codices, that was so that no one would read according to other than what the Companions had agreed upon as authentic. And something like that for a benefit is not objectionable, like washing the writing slates in the schools.
Source pages p. 1161

Commentary
This passage refutes the objection that 'Uthman burned the Quranic codices. The defense states that the burning was done to ensure uniformity and prevent people from reading in ways not agreed upon by the Companions. This act is compared to washing writing slates in schools, a common practice to prepare for new writing. The act is justified by public interest (maslahah). Thus, it is not objectionable.

Key Terms
المصاحف
إجماع الصحابة
مصلحة
غسل الألواح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Burning any Quranic text is inherently disrespectful and destructive.
Correction: The act was a justified measure to preserve the authentic Quranic text and prevent division, based on the Companions' consensus and the principle of public benefit.

Checkpoint
What is the justification given for 'Uthman burning the Quranic codices?
A) He wanted to destroy the Quran.
B) He acted on his own opinion without consultation.
C) To prevent any recitation that did not match the consensus of the Companions, similar to washing slates in schools.
D) He was angry with the scribes.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's burning of the Quranic codices was a legitimate act of preservation based on the Companions' consensus and the principle of public benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever faced criticism for a decision that you knew was correct but others misunderstood? How does the example of Uthman's action—based on consensus and benefit—challenge you to stand firm when your good intentions are not immediately clear to others?
?
When you hear about controversial actions in Islamic history, do you tend to judge them based on your own limited knowledge, or do you seek out the reasoning and context as this passage does? Reflect on a specific instance where you may have misjudged and how you can change that.
?
This passage compares burning codices to washing school slates—both acts of 'erasing' for a greater benefit. In your own life, what do you need to 'erase' (e.g., habits, assumptions, distractions) to protect what is truly important? Be specific: what one thing will you remove starting today?

Refutation of the Claim About Ammar and Uthman
Refuting two claims about Uthman and Ammar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claims that Uthman beat Ammar and that Ammar called Uthman a disbeliever.

Core Idea
The accusations against Ammar and Uthman are fabrications, rejected by Ali himself.

Sectarian disputes often involve attributing extreme statements to Companions. In this passage, the author addresses two such claims: that Uthman beat Ammar ibn Yasir, and that Ammar declared Uthman an unbeliever. He dismantles these claims by showing they are not historically established and are contradicted by the testimony of Ali and his son Hasan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then its literal English translation. Pay attention to the two distinct claims being refuted.
The author starts by dismissing the first claim as 'not established'.
2
Identify the two claims
Notice the two false statements: (1) 'Uthman beat 'Ammar, and (2) 'Ammar said 'We killed him an unbeliever.' The author rejects both.
The second claim is explicitly called a 'lie' against 'Ammar.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The author provides a narration: 'Ali disapproved of 'Ammar's statement, and al-Hasan ibn 'Ali reported that 'Ali asked 'Ammar a rhetorical question, causing him to be silent.
This narration strengthens the author's refutation.

Source Text

This indicates the statement is false.

Refuting the Claim that Uthman Exiled Abu Dharr
Refuting the claim that Uthman exiled Abu Dharr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the claim that 'Uthman exiled Abu Dharr is refuted through historical narration and rational argument.

Core Idea
The claim is unsubstantiated and even if true, could be justified.

This passage from Tabṣirat al-Adilla addresses a controversial historical claim about the Caliph 'Uthman and the Companion Abu Dharr. The author presents both a narration from al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī denying the event and a rational argument that even if it occurred, it could have been for a valid reason. This lesson examines the argument and its significance in defending the integrity of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow: first a denial, then an anecdote, then a conditional acceptance.
The passage ends abruptly; it may be incomplete in the source.
2
Analyze al-Ḥasan's response
Identify how al-Ḥasan al-Baṣrī rejected the claim: first by calling it a lie, then by explaining a possible mispronunciation (tasḥīf), and finally by saying even if true, it could be for a benefit.
The mispronunciation changes the meaning of the question.
3
Reflect on the incomplete narration
The text starts to give background on Abu Dharr's asceticism and Uthman's wealth but cuts off. Consider how this might have supported the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه نفى أبا ذر ليس بثابت أيضا، وروى أن الحسن البصري كان جالسًا في مجلسه فدخل عليه رجل فقال: أعثمان أخرج أبا ذر؟ فقال الحسن: لا. كذبوا. فتعجب أصحابه من ذلك وقالوا له: ما معنى ما دار بينكما من الكلام؟ فقال: إنه سأل على طريق التصحيف أعثمان أخرج أبا ذر؟ فقلت: لا، ولو ثبت لكان من الجائز أنه أخرجه لمصلحة رآها في ذلك. وروى أن أبا ذر [؟] كان رجلاً متزهدًا، وكان عثمان موسرا وكان يعيش
And what they claim that he exiled Abu Dharr is also not established. It is narrated that al-Hasan al-Basri was sitting in his gathering, and a man entered and said: 'Did 'Uthman expel Abu Dharr?' Al-Hasan said: 'No, they lied.' His companions were amazed by that and said: 'What is the meaning of the words that passed between you?' He said: 'He asked, with misspeaking, 'Did 'Uthman expel Abu Dharr?' and I said: 'No.' And even if it were established, it would be permissible that he expelled him for a benefit he saw in that.' And it is narrated that Abu Dharr [؟] was an ascetic man, and 'Uthman was wealthy, and he used to live...
Source pages p. 1161

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that 'Uthman exiled Abu Dharr. First, it states that the claim is not established (laysa bi thabit). Then it relates a story about al-Hasan al-Basri: a man asked him a question mispronouncing it as 'a'Uthman akhraja Aba Dharr?' (Did 'Uthman expel Abu Dharr?) and al-Hasan replied 'No, they lied.' The companions were confused until al-Hasan explained that the man mispronounced; actually the question was about 'Uthman expelling a different person, or perhaps the man said 'akhta'a' (made a mistake) but the text says 'tashif'. Al-Hasan clarifies that even if it were true, it could be for a benefit 'Uthman saw. Finally, a narration about Abu Dharr's asceticism and 'Uthman's wealth is introduced but cuts off.

Key Terms
أبو ذر
الحسن البصري
التصحيف
نفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The claim that Uthman exiled Abu Dharr is a proven historical fact.
Correction: The author demonstrates that this claim is not historically established, and even if it were, it could have been a justifiable action for a valid reason.

Checkpoint
What was al-Hasan al-Basri's response to the man who asked 'Did 'Uthman expel Abu Dharr?'
A) He said yes.
B) He said 'No, they lied.'
C) He remained silent.
D) He said 'It is none of your business.'

Key Takeaway
The claim of 'Uthman exiling Abu Dharr is historically weak and even if true, can be interpreted positively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a negative story about someone you admire, do you instinctively believe it or do you pause to question its authenticity like al-Hasan al-Basri did? What would it take for you to firmly reject such stories as 'lies' when they lack evidence?
?
Al-Hasan al-Basri gave 'Uthman the benefit of the doubt even if the story were true. Think of a recent situation where you assumed the worst about someone's intentions. How can you train yourself to seek possible valid reasons for others' actions before condemning them?
?
The phrase 'they lied' is direct and uncompromising. In what areas of your life are you hesitant to call out falsehood, especially when it comes to the honor of those who have passed? What would change if you took a stand for truth in those moments?

Why Uthman Burned the Qur'an
Nasafi refutes the accusation that Uthman burned the Qur'an.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that Uthman's burning of Qur'anic codices was a legitimate and beneficial act.

Core Idea
Uthman's burning of codices was a protective measure, not an act of destruction.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses a controversial historical accusation against Caliph Uthman. He provides a rational defense by explaining the purpose behind the burning and draws a simple analogy to illustrate its acceptability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the purpose Nasafi gives for the burning
Find the clause that states the reason for burning the codices. Notice that it is to prevent people from reading variants not agreed upon by the Companions.
3
Consider the analogy and why it strengthens the argument
Reflect on the analogy of washing school tablets. Ask yourself how this comparison defends Uthman's action as beneficial and not objectionable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه أحرق المصاحف فذلك لئلا يقرأ إنسان بغير ما أجمعت الصحابة على ثبوته ومثل ذلك لمصلحة ما، لا يكون منكرا كغسل الألواح في المكاتب
And what they claim that he burned the codices, that is so that no one might read other than what the Companions agreed upon its confirmation. And like that for a benefit, it is not objectionable, like washing the tablets in the school.
Source pages p. 1161

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that Uthman burned the Qur'an. The author states that the burning was done to prevent people from reading variant readings that were not agreed upon by the Companions. Thus, it served a legitimate purpose (maslaha). He analogizes it to wiping slate boards clean in a children's school, which is not considered wrong. The argument is that the action was beneficial for preserving the authenticity of the Qur'an.

Key Terms
أحرق المصاحف
مصلحة
إجماع الصحابة
كغسل الألواح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that Uthman burned the Qur'an out of disrespect or to alter the text.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that the burning was done to prevent confusion and ensure that only the agreed-upon Qur'anic text was read.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why did Uthman burn the codices?
A) To destroy evidence of errors
B) To prevent reading of non-consensus variants
C) To promote his own version
D) As a sign of disrespect

Key Takeaway
Uthman's burning of codices was a protective measure to unify the Qur'anic text based on the Companions' consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn that Uthman burned Qur'an copies, does your heart trust the Companions' collective wisdom more than your own unease? What would it take for you to accept a seemingly drastic decision made for a greater good?
?
This passage argues that even destructive acts can be beneficial if guided by consensus and sincere intent. Is there a difficult decision in your own life that you have avoided because it appears harsh, but might actually serve a vital purpose? Why have you resisted it?
?
The analogy of washing school tablets suggests that clearing away what is outdated is necessary to preserve what truly matters. What beliefs, habits, or attachments in your own life need to be 'washed away' to preserve your spiritual health and focus on the truth?

Refuting Accusations Against Uthman: The Case of Ammar ibn Yasir
Nasafi refutes two accusations: Uthman hitting Ammar and Ammar calling him an unbeliever.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes two false accusations against Uthman regarding Ammar ibn Yasir, using historical and textual evidence.

Core Idea
Claims about Uthman hitting Ammar are not proven; the reported statement is a lie.

In this passage, Nasafi addresses two specific sectarian accusations against Caliph Uthman. He first dismisses the report that Uthman physically struck the Companion Ammar ibn Yasir. He then refutes the claim that Ammar called Uthman an unbeliever after the incident. Nasafi supports his refutation with a narration from Ali ibn Abi Talib and his son al-Hasan, showing that Ammar remained silent when challenged, indicating the accusation's falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Nasafi directly states the first claim is 'not established'.
غير ثابت means not proven.
2
Identify the two accusations
Find the two things 'they claim' and how Nasafi responds to each. The first is about physical hitting; the second is about Ammar's statement.
The second claim is explicitly called a lie.
3
Understand the supporting narration
Examine the story of Ali and al-Hasan ibn Ali. How does this narration strengthen Nasafi's argument? What does Ammar's silence indicate?
The silence is interpreted as implied agreement with Ali's rebuke.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Ask why Nasafi might include this refutation. Consider the sectarian context. What is the overall objective?
This defends Uthman's character and refutes false historical claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما يزعمون أنه ضرب عمارا فغير ثابت وما يزعمون أن عمارا قال: قتلناه كافرًا، كذب على عمار لم يثبت ذلك عنه، وروي أن عليا أنكر ذلك على عمار، وكذا الحسن بن علي حين روى أن عليا قال له: أتكفر برب كان يؤمن به عثمان؟ فسكت عمار

And it is narrated that Ali disapproved of that from Ammar, and likewise al-Hasan ibn Ali when he narrated that Ali said to him: do you disbelieve in a Lord that Uthman believed in? Source pages p. 1161

Commentary
This passage refutes two accusations against Uthman: that he physically struck Ammar ibn Yasir, and that Ammar then called him an unbeliever. Nasafi states that the report of the beating is unsubstantiated. As for Ammar's alleged statement 'We killed him as an unbeliever', this is a fabrication. Furthermore, Nasafi reports that Ali ibn Abi Talib rebuked Ammar for such talk, and al-Hasan ibn Ali also reported that Ali asked Ammar if he disbelieved in God whom Uthman believed in, and Ammar remained silent. This silence implies Ammar's retraction or acknowledgment of error. The overall argument is that the accusations are false and the true narrations from the Ahl al-Bayt refute them.

Correction: Nasafi demonstrates that these reports are not established and are contradicted by authentic narrations from the Ahl al-Bayt.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes the claims against Uthman by declaring them unestablished and using a narration where Ammar's silence implies the falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Ammar remained silent when Ali corrected him. In your life, when someone points out a mistake in your belief or action, do you argue or humbly accept? What would change if you adopted Ammar's response of silence and reflection?
?
The false accusations against Uthman have persisted for centuries. Think of a false belief you once held about Islam or a Muslim. What did it take to let go of it? How can you ensure you only accept reports that are firmly established? Take a step today to seek knowledge from trusted sources.
